  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_949f0976-fee9-415d-931a-04b72b8560c8'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);
    };
    if (typeof AppleID !== "undefined") {
      initAppleId();
    } else {
      initializeSiwa();
    }

      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer821987250853115173">يقول الأستاذ جلال أمين عن هذه الرواية المتميزة: ها هى رواية علاء الأسوانى الجديدة «شيكاجو» تستحق بدورها نجاحًا مماثلاً وبنفس القدر من الجدارة كالذى استحقته عمارة يعقوبيان. فرحت عند انتهائى من قراءتها لأكثر من سبب، فقد أكدت لى هذه القراءة أن لدينا بالفعل أديبًا كبيرًا وموهوبًا، وظهر أن عمارة يعقوبيان ليست ظاهرة منفردة لا تتكرر، بل إن من الممكن أن تتكرر المرة بعد المرة. فى ا</span>
  <span id="freeText821987250853115173" style="display:none">يقول الأستاذ جلال أمين عن هذه الرواية المتميزة: ها هى رواية علاء الأسوانى الجديدة «شيكاجو» تستحق بدورها نجاحًا مماثلاً وبنفس القدر من الجدارة كالذى استحقته عمارة يعقوبيان. فرحت عند انتهائى من قراءتها لأكثر من سبب، فقد أكدت لى هذه القراءة أن لدينا بالفعل أديبًا كبيرًا وموهوبًا، وظهر أن عمارة يعقوبيان ليست ظاهرة منفردة لا تتكرر، بل إن من الممكن أن تتكرر المرة بعد المرة. فى الرواية الجديدة (شيكاجو) كل مزايا الرواية السابقة: التشويق الذى يبدأ من أول صفحة ويستمر إلى آخر صفحة، أسلوب الكتابة السلس والسريع الذى يصيب الهدف باستمرار بلا تثاقل أو تسكع، الرسم الواضح والمتسق للشخصيات، اللغة العربية الراقية دون تكلف أو تعمد الإغراب، وقبل كل شيء وفوق كل شيء، نُبل المعني، إذ لا جدوى فى رأيى من رواية مهما كانت درجة تشويقها وإتقانها إذا لم تكن نبيلة المقصد، وإذا كان المقصد تافهًا أو حقيرًا قضى على ما قد يكون للمهارة والشطارة من أثر فى نفس القارئ<br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.titaniumstores.com/dp/9770919403">http://www.titaniumstores.com/dp/9770...</a></span>

  <span id="freeText5512710366959260851" style="display:none">I really liked this book! I found this book by accident, and Mr. al Aswany is just a beautiful writer. The conversation flows and by the end of the book, I wasn't ready to let go of these characters. I've always been interested in the immigrant experience and to watch it unfold among people from Egypt, living in my hometown was really fascinating. I'm an expatriate living overseas in Europe now so I can really identify with these people's lives on a lot of levels -- but not all. His intimate knowledge of Chicago's neighborhoods is very good as well. I highly recommend it -- it will teach you a lot about yourself and human nature.</span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/147827187?book_show_action=true">Feb 12, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Judith" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Judith" href="/user/show/301768-judith">Judith</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer147827187" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6477544820414645797">This book is amazing on its own merits, but the circumstances surrounding my reading of the book are very fortuitous and worth mentioning. I spent the first 2 weeks of February in Chicago due to a medical emergency (not mine) and I ran out of books to read so I bought this one at Borders because it looked interesting. Meanwhile, during this time the historic events in Egypt were unfolding. As it happens, "Chicago" is a story of Egyptians in Chicago, and much of it concerns the discontent of thos</span>
  <span id="freeText6477544820414645797" style="display:none">This book is amazing on its own merits, but the circumstances surrounding my reading of the book are very fortuitous and worth mentioning. I spent the first 2 weeks of February in Chicago due to a medical emergency (not mine) and I ran out of books to read so I bought this one at Borders because it looked interesting. Meanwhile, during this time the historic events in Egypt were unfolding. As it happens, "Chicago" is a story of Egyptians in Chicago, and much of it concerns the discontent of those Egyptian citizens regarding the dictatorship. The book was first published in October of 2009, but it is perhaps even more significant today. <br /><br />The author is more famous for "The Yacoubian Building" which I attempted to read and discarded before finishing. It was hard to believe it was the same author. This book is funny, sad, romantic, sexy, suspenseful, dramatic, and very interesting. The story follows several Egyptian professors at a prestigious university and several Egyptian students at that same school. Some of the characters have been in the States for 20-30 years, and other just arrived. The perspectives of each are so interesting. My rating is 4 instead of 5 only because I thought some of the characters' endings were contrived. It was as if the editors told the author that he had to close out each story without leaving any loose ends. Otherwise this was an entertaining and informative book which just couldn't be more timely. <br /> </span>
  <a data-text-id="6477544820414645797" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_38277276" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/38277276" />
    <a title="Lara" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1021604-lara"><img alt="Lara" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1206459516p2/1021604.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/38277276?book_show_action=true">Nov 20, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lara" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lara" href="/user/show/1021604-lara">Lara</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780061452567" href="/book/show/2919274-chicago">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1021604-lara?shelf=for-harper-collins">for-harper-collins</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer38277276" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13754979555624783654">It’s a bit appalling that I haven’t written about Chicago by now, because I actually finished it a while ago. My reluctance to write has nothing to do with my impression of the book, because I must say, I really liked it - more than I expected to. Not in a “I can’t part with this book” sort of way, but I really did enjoy it. I read some reviews of the book after finishing it and was disappointed to see that it averaged about 3 stars. I realize that I have a tendency to looooove most books - to g</span>
  <span id="freeText13754979555624783654" style="display:none">It’s a bit appalling that I haven’t written about Chicago by now, because I actually finished it a while ago. My reluctance to write has nothing to do with my impression of the book, because I must say, I really liked it - more than I expected to. Not in a “I can’t part with this book” sort of way, but I really did enjoy it. I read some reviews of the book after finishing it and was disappointed to see that it averaged about 3 stars. I realize that I have a tendency to looooove most books - to give out five star ratings and announce that a book was the best one I’ve read in a particular year - but 3? Come on, people. This one’s a 4-star-er. Easily.<br /><br />I gather that a lot of people didn’t like it because they expected a lot more from it - that they expected it to be weightier. One reader suggested he or she expected it to be similar to an Orhan Pahmuk novel in some way. To that reader and to the others, I say POO. (Dudes, I tried reading one of Pahmuk’s…I think it was Istanbul…and damn if I didn’t have to add it to the very short list of books I eventually abandoned before finishing.)<br /><br />Alaa Al Aswany’s Chicago was both fun and significant. In a weird way, it resonated with me in a similar manner as my beloved Tales of the City books. I say “in a weird way” because Chicago has nothing to do with homosexuality - but there are similarities there nonetheless.<br /><br />Chicago is the story of several Egyptian students and faculty members (as well as a couple Americans) at the University of Illinois in Chicago hematology program. You’d think that I would be able to tell you by now what hematology is, but no. I am too lazy to look it up. But it involves cells and science and, um, stuff.<br /><br />Hi! I’m a genius!<br /><br />Anyway, the book follows the lives of these students and faculty members, alternating story lines in a way that sounds confusing but really isn’t. It had that same sort of cliffhanger aspect that Tales of the City has…just with different unknowns hanging in the balance. Will the conservative, veiled Muslim Egyptian woman ditch her society’s customs and make out with the nerd-ish guy who, until he met her, spent every evening home alone with no social life? Will the militant dude who is the head of the Egyptian Student Union (or whatever it’s called) flunk out of school? Will the professor who had previously completely renounced his Egyptian heritage (or, at least, its customs) continue to act like a bonehead about his daughter moving in with her boyfriend? What will happen next??<br /><br />This is all my way of saying that this was a fun, interesting read. And it’s not a total soap opera, either - it provides a fascinating glimpse into a culture to which I’ve otherwise not been exposed.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13754979555624783654" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer48413436" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer15414984899143144321">Like in a chess game, Dr. Al-Aswani kills all of the players and keeps the last two(not literally). He did the same thing in his first novel.<br/><br/>If books were rated, I’d rate this one “R” for nudity and sexual scenes….and I’m not sure if Dr. Al-Aswani is obsessed with sexual scenes, or he just enjoys dissecting his characters down to the core exposing every detail of their private moments.<br/><br/>For a moment, I thought perhaps the writer took a crash course in psychiatry before writing this novel. I was ve</span>
  <span id="freeText15414984899143144321" style="display:none">Like in a chess game, Dr. Al-Aswani kills all of the players and keeps the last two(not literally). He did the same thing in his first novel.<br /><br />If books were rated, I’d rate this one “R” for nudity and sexual scenes….and I’m not sure if Dr. Al-Aswani is obsessed with sexual scenes, or he just enjoys dissecting his characters down to the core exposing every detail of their private moments.<br /><br />For a moment, I thought perhaps the writer took a crash course in psychiatry before writing this novel. I was very impressed by his ability to present and explain what was going through the mind of each character.<br /> <br />With this novel, Dr. Al-Aswani provides a proof that he can write about anything he wants; history, politics, religion, relationships, ….etc.<br /><br />What I did not like in this novel?<br /><br />1.	Too many main characters.<br /><br />2.	Religion- the use of Islamic believes and the Quranic verses in a sarcastic way.<br /><br />3.	Irony- Nagi, who his name means “the survival” or the “rescued one”, is full of irony. He’s Muslim yet he drinks heavily, and doesn’t mind seeking a prostitute. His best friend is Coptic Christian, and his girl friend is Jewish, and he has strong patriotic feelings towards Egypt. At the end he was picked up by the FBI as a terrorist suspect….and I’m still not sure why he was the only character who was given the voice of a first person; everyone else was as a second or third person.<br /><br />4.	Exaggeration-<br />-Sarah dying of addiction because her parents did not really love each other, and b/c her father failed to build a clear inner sense of identity, was a bit hard to absorb.<br /><br />-Marawa’s fantasy “…with whom am I going to sleep tonight “was overbaord…but what do I know? Probably Dr. Al-Aswani knows better regarding women’s fantasies…yet he doesn’t seem very well updated with today’s celebrities. Rushdy Abaza died in 1980, long before G.Bush the father became a president in 1993…So I’d imagine that today’s 20-30 years old girl would fantasize about someone like Amro Diab,Tamir Hosni, or Hani Salama.<br /><br />-Carol, a black American who could not find a job, not even as a baby sitter or to walk the dogs, was unrealistic.<br /><br /><br /><br /><br />Still I think Dr. Al-Aswani worked hard on this novel, and I was impressed.<br /><br />I’d like to see him writing his next novel about one or at most two main characters.<br /><br /><br /><br /></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer77955510" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4346156581475471263">In this book, Alaa Al Aswany seems to be responding to what some people have called "the American dream" which appears in the novel to be, in fact, an American nightmare. A while ago, one of work mates was telling me he wished to travel to America "where all dreams come true and all the impossible can be rendered possible." The characters in this novel had the same thought, and they travelled to America fishing for their dreams. They face the severe problem of conflicting cultures; they are tota</span>
  <span id="freeText4346156581475471263" style="display:none">In this book, Alaa Al Aswany seems to be responding to what some people have called "the American dream" which appears in the novel to be, in fact, an American nightmare. A while ago, one of work mates was telling me he wished to travel to America "where all dreams come true and all the impossible can be rendered possible." The characters in this novel had the same thought, and they travelled to America fishing for their dreams. They face the severe problem of conflicting cultures; they are totally incapable of getting rid of their culture which someone like Dr Ra'fat describes as "retarded," but at the same time they cannot fully absorb the American culture. This is what Dr Ra'fat becomes fully aware of when his daughter decides to leave with her boyfriend and becomes a drug-addict. The novel seems to open our eyes to the reality of life in America which many people think to be the Heaven on earth. <br /><br />Merged review:<br /><br />In this book, Alaa Al Aswany seems to be responding to what people have called "the American dream" which appears in the novel to be, in fact, an American nightmare. A while ago, one of work mates was telling me he wished to travel to America "where all dreams come true and all the impossible can be rendered possible." The characters in this novel had the same thought, and they travelled to America fishing for their dreams. They face the severe problem of conflicting cultures; they are totally incapable of getting rid of their culture which someone like Dr Ra'fat describes as "retarded," but at the same time they cannot fully absorb the American culture. This is what Dr Ra'fat becomes fully aware of when his daughter decides to leave with her boyfriend and becomes a drug-addict. The novel seems to open our eyes to the reality of life in America which many people think to be the Heaven on earth.</span>

  <span id="freeText7663150390435799493" style="display:none">I thought this book would be a great experience because there are things about it that are very familar. But it's too stiff. I'm not sure if it's the translation, but no americans speak as they do in this book. It also lacks depth. The female characters are unconvincing. Also, Aswany tends to be too hyperbolic: example, "marshal fields is the best store in all of chicago." Lastly, the sex scenes reminded me of smut. Sex is fine when it's invoked for a purpose. If the goal was shock the result was cheap. All this made me long for someone like Rushdie, whom I recognize is not Egyptian. But i guess i'd prefer his arduous examinations to superficial observation. <br />The best part of chicago was Aswany's physical description of the president, which reminded me of other contemporary despots. Pretty goood-in fact, the political opinions were pretty interesting. </span>

<div id="review_2423523700" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

    <a title="Ram" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2220787-ram"><img alt="Ram" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1343648140p2/2220787.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2423523700?book_show_action=true">Jun 13, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ram" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ram" href="/user/show/2220787-ram">Ram</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2423523700" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4142385515351817169">A book about Egyptian immigrants and students in Chicago in the beginning of the 21st century.<br/><br/>The main characters of the book are postgraduate students in the University of Illinois at Chicago. <br/><br/>They are confronted with Racial discrimination, conflict between cultures and, most of all the threat of the Egyptian secret intelligence agency that is monitoring the loyalty of the Egyptian citizens in it's corrupt and threatening way.<br/><br/>The Egyptian students find themselves in an unfamiliar environment wi</span>
  <span id="freeText4142385515351817169" style="display:none">A book about Egyptian immigrants and students in Chicago in the beginning of the 21st century.<br /><br />The main characters of the book are postgraduate students in the University of Illinois at Chicago. <br /><br />They are confronted with Racial discrimination, conflict between cultures and, most of all the threat of the Egyptian secret intelligence agency that is monitoring the loyalty of the Egyptian citizens in it's corrupt and threatening way.<br /><br />The Egyptian students find themselves in an unfamiliar environment with freedom to do many things that are not acceptable in Egypt: <br />They can dress as they want, have open relationships before marriage, criticize the government and generally behave in a way that in Egypt would be considered anti governmental and anti religious. <br />This behavior could lead to social damage in Egypt and in many cases actual physical damage or criminal charges. <br /><br />While in Chicago they are officially free to behave as they like, they are still monitored and tracked by representatives of the Egyptian government who manage to influence them using various methods that include social threats and threats to them or their loved ones in Egypt. In addition they have their personal conflicts with their religious beliefs and there social conservative upbringing. <br /><br />This is the second book I read from this author and, as in the other book I read,<br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/128711.The_Yacoubian_Building" title="The Yacoubian Building by Alaa Al Aswany" rel="nofollow noopener"> The Yacoubian Building</a><br />He walks the thin line between criticism of the regime and it's brutal behavior and love of Egypt, the culture, the society and the Egyptian people.<br /> <br />While Alaa Al Aswany does not represent all Egyptians, I do believe that he has the courage to talk about many issues that are on the mind of the people and are usually not presented in such a blunt way. <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="4142385515351817169" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sarah" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sarah" href="/user/show/2422887-sarah">Sarah</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1093797169" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer808991747466894612">More Egyptians behaving badly, but this time away from home. This is really quite bad, with the most appalling stiff dialogue and laughably unrealistic situations - and it's not really set in Chicago, but in some parallel universe where they also make Egyptian soap operas.<br/><br/>I liked <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/128711.The_Yacoubian_Building' title='The Yacoubian Building by Alaa Al Aswany' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Yacoubian Building</a>, despite it having all the same issues, and like that one, this has some redeeming quality in the extreme humanity that underlies it. All of these horrible people are allowed their faults, not bec</span>
  <span id="freeText808991747466894612" style="display:none">More Egyptians behaving badly, but this time away from home. This is really quite bad, with the most appalling stiff dialogue and laughably unrealistic situations - and it's not really set in Chicago, but in some parallel universe where they also make Egyptian soap operas.<br /><br />I liked <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/128711.The_Yacoubian_Building" title="The Yacoubian Building by Alaa Al Aswany" rel="nofollow noopener">The Yacoubian Building</a>, despite it having all the same issues, and like that one, this has some redeeming quality in the extreme humanity that underlies it. All of these horrible people are allowed their faults, not because of any traumatic backstory that excuses them, but just because people do terrible things and that's how it is. On the other hand, it's misogynist, and has some weird ideas about race relations in America.<br /><br />And as with The Yacoubian Building, <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[only one unlikely couple emerges from the chaos intact and hopeful <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> but we're not as engaged with them this time. I feel these interlocked stories could be pretty interesting if toned down considerably... <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[seriously, an educated black woman in Chicago can find no job at all, not even dog walking (the worst of all possible jobs, apparently, to Al Aswany) 'because she's black', but then despite having an average figure, gets taken up as a lingerie model (for a gig which conveniently doesn't show her head and therefore she can hide it from her partner) and then foot model and paid $1000 an hour which solves all her problems (for a while) <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>.... ok, yes, land of opportunity if you're willing to prostitute yourself, but come on, man. <br /><br />Anyway, kind of interesting for the Egyptian view(s) of America, but also excruciating. </span>

<div id="review_2311493449" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2311493449" />
    <a title="Dramatika" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/11407801-dramatika"><img alt="Dramatika" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1583938856p2/11407801.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2311493449?book_show_action=true">Feb 28, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dramatika" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dramatika" href="/user/show/11407801-dramatika">Dramatika</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780061452567" href="/book/show/2919274-chicago">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11407801-dramatika?shelf=egyptian-literature">egyptian-literature</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2311493449" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1381512335705303459">Despite many shortcomings, I really enjoyed this book. The author painted a very nuanced still life on the life of Egyptian emigrants and expats living in Chicago. Tolstoy said that all unhappy family are unhappy in their own ways, and same can be said about unhappy dysfunctional countries. As a patriot of one such country I can understand the pain and suffering of the people who left, their survivor's guilt, even their enhanced hatred for their homeland. The author presented that ambiguity of e</span>
  <span id="freeText1381512335705303459" style="display:none">Despite many shortcomings, I really enjoyed this book. The author painted a very nuanced still life on the life of Egyptian emigrants and expats living in Chicago. Tolstoy said that all unhappy family are unhappy in their own ways, and same can be said about unhappy dysfunctional countries. As a patriot of one such country I can understand the pain and suffering of the people who left, their survivor's guilt, even their enhanced hatred for their homeland. The author presented that ambiguity of emigrant trying desperately to rid himself of the backwardness of their homeland, attempts to fully embrace the new progressive happy ways of their adopted one. Some become fervent advocate for the new home, rejecting wholeheartedly the old one. <br />Then there are the new expats, not permanent but temporary transfers hoping to use the opportunity to further their studies in US to provide better living back home, or closer to Egypt as in Emirates states. The graduate students narrative is the best in the book, closer to full body characters unlike the weak story line on US natives (which is ridiculous at times). There are the political poet who just uses his tine to earn good degree to provide some income not for science but for poetry writing. His narrative is the only first person POV, but I found him not that sympathetic. I really liked the shy Egyptian young woman love story, even though it is clearly meant to be a cautionary tale (the author is a man after all). The duped housewife who found her strength to teach her abusive husband a lesson is another untypical portrait of modern Egyptian woman. <br />Some parts of the book are a dark reminder why Egypt, a land blessed with so much beauty and wonderful people, is still suffering and why so many people are struggling to leave. </span>
  <a data-text-id="1381512335705303459" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2450073520?book_show_action=true">Jul 31, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ryan Furlong" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ryan Furlong" href="/user/show/69705717-ryan-furlong">Ryan Furlong</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780061452581" href="/book/show/6931262-chicago">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2450073520" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4007035969029307276">Honestly, it's a huge relief to finish this novel. I am quite frankly proud and surprised that I was able to power through this mess of a book, instead of throwing it down in search of something better. <br/>Don't get me wrong, <i>Chicago</i> was an interesting read, as the author shared insight to a culture I am increasingly curious about from the perspective of Egyptians transplanted into a Western society. This parallel allowed Aswany to critique the political and gender based inequalities that are appar</span>
  <span id="freeText4007035969029307276" style="display:none">Honestly, it's a huge relief to finish this novel. I am quite frankly proud and surprised that I was able to power through this mess of a book, instead of throwing it down in search of something better. <br />Don't get me wrong, <i>Chicago</i> was an interesting read, as the author shared insight to a culture I am increasingly curious about from the perspective of Egyptians transplanted into a Western society. This parallel allowed Aswany to critique the political and gender based inequalities that are apparent in Arab traditions, while still showing pride in the culture through his various character's personas. However, this is what also lead to the author's utter failure for this novel. Simply put, there was just way too much going on, which made it difficult to follow each plot line - It took me about 200 pages just to get the names straight. Furthermore, with lack of connection between stories, I continued to just be lost each time the book cycled back to a character. The book would have definitely had a greater impact on me if Aswany had chosen to focus on just two or three of the storylines. To be fair, I think the only reason why I kept reading was to find out what happened to the handful of characters I actually cared about. I do not think I will recommend this one to friends!</span>
  <a data-text-id="4007035969029307276" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_46734297" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/46734297?book_show_action=true">Feb 18, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Christina" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Christina" href="/user/show/565777-christina">Christina</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/565777-christina?shelf=2009">2009</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/565777-christina?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer46734297" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11748179829300460520">This was a different read. It takes place in Chicago, between faculty members and students at The University of Illinois Medical School - a lot of the characters being Egyptian muslims.<br/>We follow a group of these - some rooting for change in Egypt, others only wan to forget and move on with their life in the US.<br/>It took me a while to get into this book - it took me a lot of pages to understand how the author wrote the book, so that we follow different characters in the same circle, living kind of </span>
  <span id="freeText11748179829300460520" style="display:none">This was a different read. It takes place in Chicago, between faculty members and students at The University of Illinois Medical School - a lot of the characters being Egyptian muslims.<br />We follow a group of these - some rooting for change in Egypt, others only wan to forget and move on with their life in the US.<br />It took me a while to get into this book - it took me a lot of pages to understand how the author wrote the book, so that we follow different characters in the same circle, living kind of similar lives but very different depending on their faith and the circumstances life has given them - but without much of a red thread to keep it all togehter, except for a resistance against the Egyptian president.<br />My favourite character was Shaymaa Muhammadi who comes to the US to study and who is in her 30s without being married. She is a devout muslim woman who've always behaved like she should - but in the US she falls in love and starts to question the way she was brought up. Especially after she finds a fellow student she would like to marry...<br />I also liked the woman whose man slapped her and since she already had realized that she didn't like him very much, she wants an divorce and she's sure her parents will help her - but finds out that divorce is not even something you speak about so she has to try and make it work - 'a wise wife has to patiently put up with the shortcomings of her husband', as her mother says.<br />And what about the father who's daughter suddenly wants to move out with her boyfriend - whom the father doesn't like - will he accept their relationship or will he resort to his old ways and force her away?<br />And they all live in a post-9/11 world which makes it even harder to be an Arab immigrant in the US. <br />The characters are what drives this book - when you get them sorted out and find out who's who, you really want to know what happens to them and with them - and then the different culture and the details about Egyptian society and muslim ways add the extra touch that really makes this worth reading.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11748179829300460520" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2052011127" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2052011127" />
    <a title="Jennifer Mikhail" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/18276463-jennifer-mikhail"><img alt="Jennifer Mikhail" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1363050791p2/18276463.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2052011127?book_show_action=true">Jul 07, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jennifer Mikhail" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jennifer Mikhail" href="/user/show/18276463-jennifer-mikhail">Jennifer Mikhail</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780061452567" href="/book/show/2919274-chicago">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2052011127" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8323848834628667710">Starts off strong, introducing us to a cast of characters with varying personalities and circumstances, but all with one common trait -- their Egyptian heritage, and their experience living it out in the US. Al-Aswany employs a similar tactic as he used in yaqoubian building -- introducing us to each character separately, until (some of) their lives begin to intertwine. He uses the art of suspense masterfully -- leaving us at the end of many chapters at a cliff hanger in the depiction of his cha</span>
  <span id="freeText8323848834628667710" style="display:none">Starts off strong, introducing us to a cast of characters with varying personalities and circumstances, but all with one common trait -- their Egyptian heritage, and their experience living it out in the US. Al-Aswany employs a similar tactic as he used in yaqoubian building -- introducing us to each character separately, until (some of) their lives begin to intertwine. He uses the art of suspense masterfully -- leaving us at the end of many chapters at a cliff hanger in the depiction of his characters, and we have read through several chapters before we know what happens next with the character. <br /><br />But then the story starts to wander off in other directions, exploring the lives of Americans with no links to Egypt. One theme that features prominently is racism, no doubt a real problem in America. The problem is there's plenty of literature on this subject. <br />Al-Aswany would have been better off focusing on a theme that's rarely explored in American or Arabic literature-- the experience of arabs in the west. Unfortunately, the development of some of his characters was sacrificed at the expense of superfluous characters such as graham and carol and chris. For example, ra'fat's story is not very compelling as we don't ever see the origins of his struggle with his daughter Sarah and where things started to go wrong. Again, the focus should have been on Egyptian characters, not American. <br /><br />Second, there are inaccuracies and lack of nuance in the depiction of some aspects, for example the cooperation between American and Egyptian intelligence services, and more broadly the influence of Egyptian security in America. Such wild inaccuracies make me wonder whether Al-Aswany has had any direct experience with the subjects on which he writes, or whether he is just interested in using a broad brush to paint the people he sees as villains without offering more subtle shades. <br /><br />After reading the masterpiece yaqoubian building, this book was a disappointment. </span>
  <a data-text-id="8323848834628667710" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_50803444" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/50803444" />
    <a title="Caroline  " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/33079-caroline"><img alt="Caroline  " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1352007637p2/33079.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/50803444?book_show_action=true">Mar 29, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Caroline  " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Caroline  " href="/user/show/33079-caroline">Caroline  </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9789774161100" href="/book/show/2047599.Chicago">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/33079-caroline?shelf=prose">prose</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/33079-caroline?shelf=novel">novel</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer50803444" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7798417728196130683">I picked this up from the library on a whim, because the cover and premise looked interesting. It's a translated Egyptian novel, about life in Chicago mostly from the perspective of Egyptian students and emigres. <br/><br/>Overall, this was pretty engaging and it was instructive (and often depressing) to see how life in America (under Bush) is/was viewed by a liberal writer from a Middle Eastern culture. It also reminded me, as did reading 'White Tiger' last year, that I don't know very much about the int</span>
  <span id="freeText7798417728196130683" style="display:none">I picked this up from the library on a whim, because the cover and premise looked interesting. It's a translated Egyptian novel, about life in Chicago mostly from the perspective of Egyptian students and emigres. <br /><br />Overall, this was pretty engaging and it was instructive (and often depressing) to see how life in America (under Bush) is/was viewed by a liberal writer from a Middle Eastern culture. It also reminded me, as did reading 'White Tiger' last year, that I don't know very much about the internal politics of other countries unless they happen to be featured on NPR when I happen to be listening. Al Aswany is very critical of President Mubarak and his administration's relationship with the U.S., but I had to google to find out why. This is one of the of the values of reading international fiction, to me, once I get over being embarrassed by all the things I don't pay attention to.<br /><br />As a novel, this was just okay. There are a lot of different point of view characters who are sketched without much depth, and there's more a focus on personal melodrama (one character has a drug-addicted daughter, another is impotent with his American wife because he misses his true love back in Egypt), and when the story moves away from Egyptian emigres to American characters, it feels particularly forced. The whole thing seems very well-intentioned -- there's a lot of bad treatment of women told from the female point of view, and apparently meant to make us aware that women are often treated badly, but it's mostly just unpleasant to read without offering much in the way of insight. I'd have liked to see a few of the storylines fleshed out more, particularly the political intrigues.<br /><br />Eye-opening, in a lot of ways, but not nearly what it could have been as literature.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7798417728196130683" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer833635106" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2876229715439670021">This book made me frown, a lot. <br/>I'm not a big fan of blatant disrespect for women, (how’s sexual assault for a good read!)But hey as long as it’s in pursuit of a good character then go for it.<br/>But this was my problem; I found the characters to be abhorrent. Some had charm, I'll admit, and I was totally convinced by them, but I have never been so repulsed. I felt like the book was actively trying to hate me. Which makes no sense, but the more I read the more I wanted to stop reading, but I commit </span>
  <span id="freeText2876229715439670021" style="display:none">This book made me frown, a lot. <br />I'm not a big fan of blatant disrespect for women, (how’s sexual assault for a good read!)But hey as long as it’s in pursuit of a good character then go for it.<br />But this was my problem; I found the characters to be abhorrent. Some had charm, I'll admit, and I was totally convinced by them, but I have never been so repulsed. I felt like the book was actively trying to hate me. Which makes no sense, but the more I read the more I wanted to stop reading, but I commit to books, if only for a conclusion. <br />I get that this was meant to be one big exploration of wider themes. Racial oppression, government corruption and so on, but I feel like it came up short, a bit bland and perhaps catering to a white audience, in an attempt to... I don't even know.<br />I wasn't rooting for any character, in fact I didn't care about any of them, except perhaps the struggle of one of the women, but before I could get involved with her, the chapter ended in a grand solute to the fragmenting style, which left me back to hating the other characters until she appeared again, about 5 chapters later. <br />I gave this 2 stars only because I was convinced with the Egyptian characters. His representation of Americans is, well interesting but flat, (although I am neither American, or Egyptian, so my context in judging their believability is limited) however I was convinced in my hate for all of them, as one big unifying whole.</span>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/26947861" />
    <a title="William" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/137418-william"><img alt="William" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1577903138p2/137418.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/26947861?book_show_action=true">Jul 11, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="William" class="user" itemprop="url" name="William" href="/user/show/137418-william">William</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780007285181" href="/book/show/3651522-chicago">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/137418-william?shelf=immigration">immigration</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/137418-william?shelf=amazon-vine">amazon-vine</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/137418-william?shelf=chicago">chicago</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/137418-william?shelf=africa">africa</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/137418-william?shelf=islam">islam</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/137418-william?shelf=middle-east">middle-east</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer26947861" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16457025642376739614">Alaa Al Aswany’s novel Chicago suffers from a translation that never succeeds in allowing the dialogue to sound anything but that: translated. Speech from the novel’s Egyptian immigrant scholars might be passable if readers expect stiff and awkward phrasing, but the Chicago natives here sound equally stilted. The result isn’t completely unattractive: the overly formal speech allows Al Aswany’s many messages – of American racism toward Arabs and African-Americans, of the conflicts felt by devout </span>
  <span id="freeText16457025642376739614" style="display:none">Alaa Al Aswany’s novel Chicago suffers from a translation that never succeeds in allowing the dialogue to sound anything but that: translated. Speech from the novel’s Egyptian immigrant scholars might be passable if readers expect stiff and awkward phrasing, but the Chicago natives here sound equally stilted. The result isn’t completely unattractive: the overly formal speech allows Al Aswany’s many messages – of American racism toward Arabs and African-Americans, of the conflicts felt by devout Muslims practicing in a primarily Christian nation, of drug addiction, of infidelity, of the competing passions of morality and sensuality – to be approached with a tone of naivety that can, in places, allow these issues to be freshly viewed. The larger problem is that Chicago’s many stories, centered primarily on Egyptian Arabs studying histology at the University of Illinois Medical School, never cohere. The novel is still an enjoyable read, however. In particular, the ending hints at an emotional depth that, while not quite realized in the rest of the novel, feels satisfying nonetheless.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="16457025642376739614" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground">Readers also enjoyed</h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent"><div class='bookCarousel'>

<li class='cover' id='bookCover_17524166'>
<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/17524166"><img alt="نادي السيارات" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/books/1364918924l/17524166._SX98_.jpg" />

<script type="text/javascript" charset="utf-8">
//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('QNcUr7PZByrc2oOkDrrVvefHNCkObycOdTEEJQrQ1+d68X19CIfOQnvxN8X5T99iMZjT4cDiqWkuT0KUx5MlfQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('qkZD08kcfeIq3zOBngKrVC/C6vfnNPO4FxYDQGPwDumQYCoBckK0io30h+Bp96GL+Z0NPym5fd9MaEXxrrP8cw==')})
  }
//]]>
</script>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer9855894972855017077"><a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/4465322' rel='nofollow noopener'>Alaa Al Aswany</a><br/>علاء الأسواني ولد في بالقاهرة لعائلة برجوازية، هو ابن الأديب عباس الأسواني المحامي، الذي أحيا فن المقامات وسمّاها المقامات الأسوانية في الستينات من القرن العشرين، وحاز علی جائزة الدولة من جرّاء رواية الأسوار العالية، غير أنه لم يكن مثل ابنه ذاع صيته. علاء ينتمي نفسه إلى أسرة مثقفة لأن جده أيضاً كان شاعراً مُرتجلاً و عم والدته عُين مدة وزيراً للمعارف. يعتبر علاء أستاذه الأول أباه لأنه</span>
  <span id="freeText9855894972855017077" style="display:none"><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/4465322" rel="nofollow noopener">Alaa Al Aswany</a><br />علاء الأسواني ولد في بالقاهرة لعائلة برجوازية، هو ابن الأديب عباس الأسواني المحامي، الذي أحيا فن المقامات وسمّاها المقامات الأسوانية في الستينات من القرن العشرين، وحاز علی جائزة الدولة من جرّاء رواية الأسوار العالية، غير أنه لم يكن مثل ابنه ذاع صيته. علاء ينتمي نفسه إلى أسرة مثقفة لأن جده أيضاً كان شاعراً مُرتجلاً و عم والدته عُين مدة وزيراً للمعارف. يعتبر علاء أستاذه الأول أباه لأنه أرشده إلى الكتابة وأعطاه مكتبته الضخمة، ونشأ و ترعرع علاء في وسط هذه المكتبة التي تضم أفضل وأروع الإبداعات الإنسانية في مختلف المجالات. و تتلمذ لأصدقاء أبيه الذين كانوا يعتبرون أعلام مصر مثل إحسان عبد القدوس، وعبد الرحمان الشرقاوي، وحسن فؤاد، وصلاح جاهين ولويس جريس و.<br /><br />ونلاحظ أن الهواية الأولى له هي القراءة منذ نعومة أظافره وبسبب نصيحة والده المحامي بأن يجب عليك ألا تعتمد علی الرواية وتمتهنها ؛ لأنها ستقدم لك تنازلات كل يوم، لذلك التحق بكلية الطب بجامعة إلينوي في شيكاغو في الولايات المتحدة، وحصل علی شهادة الماجستير في طب الأسنان، وهي نقطة تحول في حياته، وعلی حد قوله: الأطباء في بلاده يحصلون علی شهادة الطب عن طريق حبهم للأدب . وبهذه الجامعة تعلم قواعد البحث العلمي والتفكير العلمي، وهو يتقن عدة اللغات منها: الفرنسية؛ لأنه درس الثانوية في مدرسة الفرنسية. والأسبانية لأنه أخذ منحته الدراسية في أسبانيا لدراسة الأدب في الحضارة الأسبانية والإنجليزية. مازال علاء الأسواني يمارس مهنته كطبيب للأسنان مبرراً ذلك بأنه لا يريد أن تُصبح الكتابة وسيلة يتكسب منها عيشه، بل هواية يتنفس ويحلم من خلالها. وهو عضو في حركة كفاية المعارضة في مصر.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9855894972855017077" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
